Et se nos volons mout de sanc, si rencomensons sovent; et se li sans est espés, si fier em parfont, et li termines si est que li keurs soit perciez soudement, et couvient a celui qui a gros cuir et dur, et qui ont estrois porelz, que tu oignes les leus des ventouses d’oile aperitif et dissolutif: se c’est en esteit, d’oile violet, ou d’oile d’amandres douces, ou d’oile de semence de cucurbite; et se c’est en yveir, d’oile de cresson, ou de lis, ou de camomille, ou autre semblant.	And if we want a lot of blood, so let us often restart it; and if the blood is thick, so let us make it thin, and the end is that the veins be pierced soundly, and it is necessary for him who has thick skin and hard, and who has hairs pores, that you anoint their places with cupping glasses of oil aperitif and dissolving: if it is in summer, of violet oil, or almond sweet oil, or seed of gourd oil; and if it is in winter, of cresson oil, or lily, or chamomile, or a similar. 
Et se li superfluitez est grosse et froide, si pren oile amaratin, ou de balsamite, ou oile d’anet, et les semblans.	And if the superfluities are large and cold, then take wormwood oil, or balm oil, or anise oil, and similar things.
Et se cil cui on saine a tendre char et larges porelz, se n’i covient mettre point d’oile, mais le covient laver aprés la ventosation d’aigue rose, ou d’aigue froide, ou d’aigue de morelle, ou d’aigue de cucurbite, ou d’aigue de porcelainne, ou autre semblant.	And if the one to whom one tends has tender flesh and large pores, one must not put any oil on it, but one must wash it after the inflation of rose water, or cold water, or mulberry water, or gourd water, or porcelain water, or something similar. 
Et cil qui a mout de moistor, si se leve d’aisil, ou d’aigue de mirte, et de sumac, et de semblans choses.	And he who has much moisture, may rise from ash, or myrtle water, and sumac, and similar things.
Et cil qui ont grosses superfluitez se levent de vin viel, ou d’aigue de seuc, ou de decoction d’anet ou de camomile, ou d’autres semblans; et ne se covient mie sainier de ventouse devant le bain ne em bain, mais aprés lou bain une houre ou II; et ne dorme mie aprés la ventosacion, et covient k’il se warse, quant il se doit ventoser et quant il est ventosez.	And those who have large superfluities, raise themselves from old wine or from their own water, or from a decoction of anise or chamomile, or others like it; and it does not become well to draw blood with leeches before the bath nor in the bath, but after the bath one or two hours; and do not sleep after the cupping, and it is necessary that he should warm himself when he must be cupped and when he is cupped.  
Et warde se cil qui se saine est coleriques, et se li sans est trop agus et enflamez; si covient qu’il preigne choses froides, si com pomes grenates et endives et laitues a l’aisil, et semblans choses; et menjuce pucins, et char de chastron, et viandes moistes.	And guard those who are recovering are choleric, and if their insides are too hot and inflamed; then it is necessary that they eat cold things, such as pomegranates and endives and lettuces at the side, and similar things; and cooked beans, and roasted meat, and moist meats. 
Et cil qui a froide complexion, si preigne syrop de miel, et vin odorant moien, qui est entre viel et noveil, et menjuce poc, et menjuce pucins et petis oiselz et pijons; et lou jor qu’il se ventousera, si doit plus boivre que mengier; et covient doner a aucuns homes aucune fois triaicle ou muscate devant la ventosacion, ou aprés, por conforter les membres principalz; et soit subtiliiez li sans, mais il ne covient mie que cil qui sont chaut le boivent.	And those who have a cold complexion, take syrup of honey, and fragrant wine, which is between old and new, and minced pear, and minced quince and small onions and pigeons; and on the day that he will be bled, he must drink more than eat; and it is necessary to give some men once triacle or musk before bleeding, or after, to comfort the main limbs; and let it be subtle, but it is not necessary for those who are hot to drink it. 
Les ventouses que sont mises sens ferir sont celles que sont mises sus lou foie, et sus la mice, et podagre, et sus lou ventre et la boude et les rains et la hainche, car cil membre ne soffrent mie ferir sus oulz, et ne le fait on fuer que por atraire sanc de membre em membre, si com nos mettons sus les mameles quant li neis sainne; por oster la viscositeit qui est au membre, si com nos metons ventouses sus le ventre et sus la boude por amenuisier le membre; et oste la dolor, quant il i a opilation ou piere, si oste par sa vertu atractive par aventure l’opilation, ou desaert la piere de son leu; et ensi fait ele quant on le met sus lou foie et sus la mice quant il i a ventositeit retenue, et ces ventouses sont aministrees vuides por sucier tant soulement; et aucune fois sont aministrees ensamble feu, et aucunes fois plainnes d’aigue tede, si com em maladies dou pleuresin, et en tel maniere c’on emplisse la ventouse d’aigue chaude soule, ou d’aigue ou il ait cuit herbes covenaubles a ceu.	The cupping glasses that are placed without hurting are those that are placed on the liver, and on the spleen, and gout, and on the belly and bladder and the hips and the groin, because these members do not suffer to be hurt on their own, and it is done only to draw blood from limb to limb, like we put them on the breasts when the child is born; to remove the viscosity that is in the limb, like we put cupping glasses on the belly and bladder to reduce the limb; and to remove the pain, when there is an abscess or a stone, so it removes by its attractive virtue perhaps the abscess, or dislodges the stone from its place; and thus it does when it is placed on the liver and spleen when there is retained wind, and these cupping glasses are administered empty to suck only so much; and sometimes they are administered together with fire, and sometimes filled with hot water, like in illnesses of the pleura, and in such a way one fills the cupping glass with hot water alone, or with water where herbs suitable for this have been cooked. 
Li forme de la ventouse aministré a feu si est ci:	The form of the cupping glass administered with fire is as follows:
Et ait II dois de large et demi palme de parfont, et ait ou costeit entor lou mileu I petit pertus, ausi com por bouter une aguille, et soit d’arain, souef devant si qu’ele ne blece lou malaide au mettre sus lou membre; et ait em mileu une verge de travers d’arain ou de fer, por mettre une chandoile emprise, et en soient et grans et petites selonc les maladies et l’eaige de ces qui les prenent; car les ventouses des enfans sont autres que des vielz, et des maigres que des gras.	And it has two parts of large and half a palm from front to back, and it has or rib around the middle small hole, as for putting in a needle, and be iron, soft in front so that it does not burn the patient when put on the member; and it has in the middle a bar across of iron or steel, to put a candle ember, and they are both large and small according to the diseases and the age of those who take them; for the cups of children are other than the old, and the thin ones than the fat. 
Et doit on ensi mettre ceste ventouse sus lou membre: empren la chandoile de cire ou I linement, et le met emmi la ventouse au traver de la verge qui est emmi la ventouse, si que li clartez soit au fons de la ventouse qu’elle ne brule le malaide; et puis la met sus lou membre, et tes dois soit sus lou pertus que nos avons dit, si que quant on vuet, si en va li vapor, et oste on la ventouse; et emprent on le linement si com devant, et le rencomence sovent, se tu as mestier.	And one must thus put this suction cup on the limb: grasp the candle of wax or linen, and place it inside the suction cup across from the rod which is inside the suction cup, so that the clarity is at the bottom of the suction cup that it does not burn the patient; and then put it on the limb, and the two ends should be on the opening that we have said, so that when one wishes, vapor goes in, and one takes off the suction cup; and grasp the linen as before, and begin again often, if you need. 
En la ventouse donques qui est aministree em maladie de pleuresin, a tout aigue, n’a point de verge ne de pertus, et n’est amenistree fuer que quant ele est plaine d’aigue, et est mise sus lou membre tant soulement; et c’est li forme:	In the suction cup therefore which is administered in illness of pleuresin, at all acute, has no rod nor pierced, and is administered only when it is full of fluid, and is put upon the member so only; and this is the form: 
Sansues ne sont amenistrees fuer que en membres ou on ne puet mettre ventouses por lour petitece, si com levres et gencives, et semblans leus; et en leus desnuez de char, si com doi ou neis, et autres semblans; et li qualitez de l’user si est que quant tu trueves sansues que sont en aigues douces nettes sens porreture, si les pren; et les met une nut en aigue douce, tant qu’elles soient famillouses, et n’aient rien en ventres; et puis soit sainiez de voinne et de ventouse, puis frote le membre tant k’il soit rouges, et met sus les sansues; et quant elles seront plainnes, et cherront, se c’est leu ou on puisse mettre ventouse, se li met, car ce vaut mout aprés.	Abscesses are brought forth only in members where it is impossible to apply leeches because of their smallness, such as lips and gums, and similar; and in those denuded of flesh, such as two or one, and others similar; and the qualities of its use are that when you find abscesses that are in clear water, no more porous, so take them; and put them in a nut in clear water, until they become familiar, and have nothing in their bellies; and then cleanse them from vein and leech cup, then rub the limb as long as it is red, and place the abscesses above; and when they are full, and fall, if it is their place where one can apply a leech cup, so put it on, because it is very good after.  
Se ceu non, si leve le leu d’aisil, et puis d’aigue, mout bien, et puis l’espreint; et se li leus saine trop aprés lou cors des sansues, si moille I drapel de lin en aigue froide, et met sus, tant que li sans soit estenchiés; et se li sans est multipliez, si met sus vitriole broié, ou gales, et semblans choses, tant qu’il soit estenchiez; ou tu met sor le leu moitiez de feves pelees, et les laisse tant qu’elles s’aerdent au leu, si estanchera maintenant; et se tu en as de plus mestier, si pren autres sansues.	If this is not the case, lift the head of the joint with a towel, then with water, well, and then tighten it; and if the skin heals too late after the body of the wound, soak the linen cloth in cold water and put it on top, until the wound is closed; and if the wounds are multiplied, put crushed vitriol or salt and similar things on top, until it is closed; or put half of peeled beans on the place and leave them until they dry out on the place, then it will stop now; and if you need more of this, take other herbs. 
Et se les sansues ne se vuellent prendre, si frote le leu de sanc fres, ou tu i fiche une aguille tant que li sans en isse, et puis les met sus, et tantost com elles sentent lou sanc, si s’aerdent; et quant tu vues qu’elles chieent, si poudre sus un poc d’aloen et de seil broiet, ou de cendre, si cherront maintenant.	And if the sutures do not want to be taken, rub the place with fresh blood, or stick a needle there until the suture is released, and then put them on top, and as soon as they feel the blood, they will harden; and when you see that they are shrinking, sprinkle on a little bit of aloes and soil ground, or ashes, and they will now hold. 
Ci comence li livre des brisures	Here begins the book of fractures
Cist chapistres est maintes fois necessaires en l’art de medicine, et est restorement de brisure et de desaluement.	This chapter is many times necessary in the art of medicine, and is the restoring of fracture and dislocation.
Saichiez, chier fil, que li fol mire se gietent de cest chapistre, qu’il ne virent onques nul des anciens livres, ne lettre n’en lurent; et por ceste cause est estraingie li science en nostre terre, car je n’en trovai onques nul boin; et n’en sai nient, fuer que par la science des anciens livres, ou j’ai tant estudiiet que je en ai traite la science, et par experiment, et par l’acostumance de ma vie; et je vos ai jai descrit en cest chapistre ceu qui est contenut, et l’ai abregié en la fin, et ai mostreit clerement les formes des estrumens que sont amenistrez, si com j’ai fait en ces II chapistres ci devant.	Know, dear friend, that the fool mire lies in this chapter, who never saw any of the ancient books, nor read a letter from them; and for this cause is science estranged in our land, because I have never found any good one; and I know nothing, except by the science of the ancient books, where I have studied so much that I have treated the science, and by experiment, and by the custom of my life; and I have shown you in this chapter what is contained, and I have abridged it at the end, and have clearly shown the forms of the instruments that are brought, as I have done in these II chapters before. 
Il covient donques que nos disons ou prologue de cest livre les sommes de la parole et les chapistres ou li saiges met son entendement, et demore sus la verteit, si que cil qui sont esproveit saichent et aprenent cest art noble, et l’aprenent a autrui.	It is therefore necessary that we say in the prologue of this book the sum of words and chapters where the sages put their understanding, and stay on the truth, so that those who are experienced know and learn this noble art, and learn it to others.
Je dis que quant brisure ou dislocation ou torsions ou quasseure vient a aucun, si covient que tu te hastes de sainier lou malaide d’une part et d’autre, s’aucune chose ne lou te tot, si com foiblece de cors, ou enfes, ou viellars; ou se li tens n’est tres chaus, ou trop frois; et menjuce cholz frois, c’est a savoir froides joutes, et char d’oiseil et de chevril, et ne boive point de vin, ne menjuce nules grasses chars, et ne s’emplisse mie trop de viandes qui remplissent les voinnes de sanc, tant que tu n’aies doute d’aposteme chaut, ne d’espandement de matiere au membre; si reviegne li malaides a som premerain governement k’il a useit.	I say that when a fracture or dislocation or torsion or sprain comes to anyone, it is necessary that you haste to heal the patient on one side and the other, if nothing prevents it, such as weakness of body, or infancy, or old age; or if his strength isn’t very warm, or too cold; and avoid cold things, that is to say cold meats, and venison and mutton, and do not drink wine, nor eat any fatty foods, and do not fill yourself too much with meats which fill the veins with blood, until you have doubt of a hot abscess, or an expansion of matter in the limb; if then the patient returns to his first rule that he has used. 
Et quant li os brisiés comence a restourer, si covient que on norrisse le malaide de viandes que font gros norrissement, ou il ait viscositeit, si com froment cuit en aigue, et ris, et testes, et piez, et pance de vaiche, et oefz, et poissons fres, et gros vin, et semblans chose.	And when the broken bone begins to heal, it is necessary that one nourish the patient with meats that make great nourishment, or which have viscosity, such as wheat cooked in water, and rice, and testicles, and pies, and tripe of veal, and eggs, and fresh fish, and red wine, and similar things.
Car a teil norrissement se reprent mues et plus tost li brisure.	For with such nourishment it regains strength and the fracture heals faster.
Et saichiez que li os brisiez des vielz et des decrepites ne se pueent mie bien reprendre ne souder selonc lor nature por la sechor des membres, ne cil ausi qui sont trop tendre, si com os de petis enfans; mais n’a cure, ramoinne sus l’os brisiet I gros tenrons qui s’aert et joint le membre ensamble, tant qu’il enforce si com il fut devant; et ne li tot point de son oevre, et por ceu le covient norrir de grosses viandes, si com nos avons dit.	And know that the bones broken of the old and decrepit do not well rejoin or heal according to their nature because of the dryness of the limbs, nor those who are too tender, like the bones of small children; but don't worry, reset on the broken bone, the large tendons that hold and join the limb together, as long as it forces as it was before; and do not point anything in its work, and for this one must nourish with thick meats, as we have said.  
Et saiches que les briseures sont diverses em maintes manieres.	And know that fractures are diverse in many ways.
Car li brisure dou chief est autre que li brisure dou pis, ou de la jambe, et je vos dirai en cest chapistre toutes manieres de brisure.	Because a fracture of the head is different from a fracture of the leg, or foot, and I will tell you in this chapter all kinds of fractures.
Car il i a brisure que vait a arvol, et autre en aguisant, si com li taille d’une penne por escrire.	Because there are fractures that go in a circle, and others in sharpening, like the point of a pen for writing.
Et si a brisure avec plaie, et brisure a tout une petite fendure.	And if there is a fracture with a wound, and a fracture all over with a small fissure.
Et a chescune maniere ait son engien, et son estrument propre en la restauration, selonc ceu que li remembrance vient en son leu; et est conue li brisure de l’os par ceu que li leus est tors, et apert bien par defuer, et sone au presseir de la main; et quant il n’i a tortuositeit ne son au tochier, et li malaides ne sent mie grant dolor, se n’i a point de brisure; mais il puet bien avoir tortuositeit, ou fendure, ou petite brisure.	And for each manner has its tool and instrument proper in restoration, according to what the remembrance comes in its place; and is known the fracture of the bone by this that it is twisted, and opened well by outside, and feels under pressure of the hand; and when there is no twist nor feels under touching, and the patient does not feel great pain, there is no point of fracture; but it can well have twisting, or tear, or small fracture. 
Si ne covient mie que tu lou mueves a la main, mais met sus medicines que vienent ci aprés, et puis l’estraint maintenant subtilment.	You should not move it by hand, but apply the medicines that come after, and then restrain it carefully.
Et saiches que li brisure que vait en arvol, et sont les II parties desjointes, il les covient maintenant mettre a point igalment, ainsois que apostemes chaus i viegne; et se apostemes chaulz i vient, si lou laisse aucun jor, tant que li apostemes soit apaisiez; puis le met igal au mues que tu pues legierement et saigement, et saiches que li restauration de li est plus legiere que li restaurations de celi ou il ait pieces; puis l’estraint, si com il covient ci aprés.	And know that the fracture that goes into a bow, and are the two parts disjointed, it is necessary now to put them equally together, so that abscesses come not; and if abscesses do come, leave none of them for any day, until the abscesses are calmed; then put it equal to the joint as you can gently and wisely, and know that the restoration of this is lighter than the restoration of that which has pieces; then restrain, as it is necessary afterward. 
Et se en l’os a piéces brisies, si l’estent de II pars, se c’est mains ou piet, ou a ta main se c’est uns petis membres, ou a II cordes, ou a une corde et a ta main; et met le membre selonc sa figure natureil, si que quant les parties dou membre brisiet sont desevrees, si ramoinne par toz engiens les additions en lor leus au mues que tu pues, et garde que par ton ovraige ne viegne au malaide lesion ne doulor; et ajoste bien les II chiés des os selonc les millors formes.	And if the bone is broken in pieces, if it’s two parts, whether it’s the hand or foot, or to your hand if it’s a small limb, or to two cords, or to one cord and to your hand; and place the limb according to its natural figure, so that when the parts of the broken member are separated, you put back together with all means the additions in their places as much as you can, and keep that by your work comes no injury or pain to the sick person; and adjust well the two heads of the bones according to the best forms.  
Et le sent bien a la main s’il te semble k’il ne soit mie bien: si la dresce bellement, et ne l’estraindre mie trop, ne presser si com font cil qui i font venir par lor ovraige aposteme chaut et contraction au membre, si com nos avons veut maintes fois.	And he feels it well in the hand if it seems to you that it is not well; if it is beautifully straightened, and do not restrain it too much, nor press as those who bring it by their work abscesses hot and contraction to the limb, as we have wanted many times.
Et quant li membres est a point, si chastie bien lou malaide qu’il ne lou mueve, ne en veillant ne en dormant, ne en tornant, ne en autre maniere; et warde bien que li membres soit si asseurez qu’il ne sente nule dolor.	And when the limb is in place, carefully instruct the patient that he does not move it, neither watching nor sleeping, nor turning, nor in any other way; and guard well that the limb be so assured that he feels no pain. 
Car quant il sent nulle dolor, si n’est mie a point, et le covient mettre en teil disposition qu’il ne sent nule dolor; et te garde k’il soit mis droitement, que tortuositez n’i veigne quant il iert restorez; et li qualitez dou membre brisiet est selonc ceu que je te dis.	For when it feels no pain, except at a point, and it behooves to put it in such a disposition that it feels no pain; and keep that it be put straight, that twistiness does not come when it is restored; and the qualities of the broken limb are according to those that I tell you.  
Saiches que li membre brisiet sont divers em petitece et en grandor et en forme.	Know that broken limbs are diverse in smallness and largeness and in form.
Car il i a petis membres, si com brais et dois et semblans: si covient envoloupeir drapelz de lin molz et subtilz; et cil qui sont gros, si com cuixe et jambe: si covient envoloupeir d’un liien large et dur, car li larges liiens embraice tout le grant membre, et l’estraint de toutes pars igalment, si qu’il n’i a nule taiche; puis lie entor estoupes legieres, III tours ou IIII selonc lou membre, et estraint ta main un poc a tout le liien, puis vai au desus de la brisure et l’estraint moins, que la brisure puis esloigne un poc la brisure; et mollifie lou liien poc et poc, tant que tu preignes grant partie dou leu sain, puis repren I autre liien, et torne entor derechief sus lou leu brisiet; puis revai a la partie desoz de la brisure, et l’estraint moienement, ne mie trop fort ne trop molement, si com nos avons dit en la premiere lieure desus; puis met entre les liiens estoupes de lin ou drapelz, tant qu’il soit toz igalz, puis lie tout par desus I bendeil, et puis met tes esteles bien faites sus, s’il n’i a aposteme ou enfleure.	For there are small limbs, such as arms and legs and similar things: it is necessary to wrap them in cloths of soft and thin linen; and those which are large, such as the hips and thighs: it is necessary to wrap them in a wide and hard bandage, because the wide bandages embrace the entire great limb, and restrain it from all sides equally, so that there is no gap; then bind above the splint light wraps, III tours or IIII according to the limb, and restrain your hand a little on the whole bandage, then go to the top of the fracture and restrain less, so that the fracture moves away a little from the fracture; and soften the bandage little by little, until you reach a large part of the healthy place, then take the other bandage, and turn it back over the broken place; then return to the bottom part of the fracture, and restrain it moderately, neither too much nor too little, as we have said in the first section above; then put between the bandages wraps of linen or cloth, so that they are all equal, then bind everything from above with a bandage, and then put your well-made splints on top, if there is no abscess or swelling. 
Et s’il i a enfleure ou aposteme, si met sus chose apaisant aposteme chaut, et qui oste l’enfleure, et le laisse ensi aucuns jors; puis estrain sus tes esteles, et soient de moitiet d’une large cainne rese saigement, ou de fust dont on fait les clives, ou de rains de palme, et soient selonc ceste figure:	And if there is a swelling or abscess, put upon it something soothing for the abscess hot, and which removes the swelling, and let it remain thus for some days; then strain upon the eyelids, and be of half a breadth of a cane well-seasoned, or of a staff from which they make the cleves, or of palm leaves, and be according to this figure:
mais il covient que li estele que om met sus la brisure soit un poc plus grosse et plus large que les autres; et soit ausi longe com il covient au membre de petitece ou de grandor; puis estraint sus les esteles ausi com tu as fait a la premiere lieure, puis lie desus un fil avenanment, si com en la premiere lieure sus la briseure; plus estroit est ensus, adés moins; et covient que li fil soient moien, ne trop gros ne trop menus, et soient de lin mol; car se li fil sont gros, si com j’ai veut des oevres des folz qui font lor lieures des cordes de linement tort, et c’est grant error, car li lieure ne puet estre igal s’il est trop subtilz, et ne covient mie qu’il ait entre chescune estele moins dou doi d’espaice.	but it is necessary that the splint that one puts on the fracture be a little larger and wider than the others; and also as long as it is suitable for the limb of smallness or bigness; then tighten on the splints as you have done at the first hour, then tie above a thread evenly, as in the first hour on the fracture; tighter it is above, less below; and it is necessary that the threads be medium, not too thick nor too thin, and be of soft linen; for if the threads are thick, as I want works of fools who do their dressings with cords of twisted linen, and this is a great error, because the dressing cannot be equal if it is too subtle, and it does not become that there be between each splint less than two finger's width. 
Et se li malaides est navrez par les chiés des esteles aprés l’estrignement en leus sains, si met desoz elles estoupes de lin, ou lainne charpie, si qu’il ne se blece.	And if the patient is bruised by the edges of the stitches after the tightening in their flesh, then put under them bandages of linen or wool, so that it does not turn blue.
Quant li plaie est avec la briseure, et li keurs est rompus, je en dirai la cure ci aprés.	When the wound is with the breaking, and the sinew is broken, I will say its cure here after.
Et saiches qu’il ne covient mie estraindre d’esteles toz les membres brisiez le premier jor.	And know that it is not necessary to set all broken limbs on the first day.
Car quant li membres est grans, se n’i covient mie mettre esteles fuer que aprés V jors ou VII, ou plus selonc ta seurteit d’aposteme chaut sorvenant; et vez ci la maniere des emplaustres anciiens por restorer brisure, dislocation, et torsion: et est que tu pren de la farine que se tient as parois dou molin, et la confis avec abun d’uef, et met ceste confection, ne trop clere ne trop espesse, et en use, et c’est li commune maniere de l’emplaustre restorant la brisure a plusors dispositions, et meismement as femmes et as petis enfans, car il n’est mie ne trop chas ne trop frois.	For when the limb is large, it is not advisable to put splints because after V days or VII, or more according to your certainty of an abscess appearing; and here is the manner of the old plasters for restoring fractures, dislocations, and torsion: and that you take flour that holds on the walls of the mill, and boil it with abundance of egg, and put this mixture, not too light nor too thick, and use it, and this is the common manner of the plaster restoring the fracture to many dispositions, and also to women and to small children, because it is not too hot nor too cold.  
Autre maniere d’emplastre restorans la brisure et la dislocation et la torsion: pren З abareis et ladani et enule et asefetide et gomme arabique et mugat, ana .x. З.; mirre et aloes, ana .v. З.; alechil .viiij. З.; et en fai poudre, et les melle avec aubun d’uef; se li complexion de l’ome est chaude, si use de cest emplaustre, car il est de bone composition, et restoure les os brisiés hastanment, et vaut a plusors homes.	Another manner of plaster restoring a fracture and dislocation and torsion: take 3 parts of  barberries and ladane and euphrasia and asafetida and gum arabic and mugwort, ana .x. З.; myrrh and aloes, ana .v. З.; and alechil .viiij. З.; and make it into powder, and mix it with the white of an egg; if the complexion of the man is hot, use this plaster, because it is of good composition, and restores broken bones well, and it is useful to many men.
Autre maniere d’emplaustre que vaut a torsion: pren mugat et vismalve, ana X З.; mirre et aloes, ana VIIII З.; acaice VI З.; bolic armenic X З., et broie bien, et confit avec aigue ou abun d’uef, et l’aministre.	Another manner of ointment that is good for torsion: take mugwort and wormwood, ana X Z.; myrrh and aloes, ana VIIII Z.; acacia VI Z.; Armenian bolus X Z., and grind well, and confit with water or the white of an egg, and administer. 
Autre maniere d’emplaustre a jointes et as os desevrez de lor leus, et apaise la dolor sorvenant, et le taille usent de l’os, et la briseure: pren lainne novele, et la moille en aisil et en oile cuit, et met sus lou leu.	Another manner of plaistering joints and bones dislocated from their places, and appeases the pain coming, and the size uses of the bone, and the fracture: take new linen, and moisten it in vinegar and oil cooked, and put on the place. 
Cist emplastres ne restoure mie, mais il est boins por apaisier d’aposteme chaut, et oste proprement les dolors.	This plaster does not restore [something], but it is good for soothing a hot abscess, and properly removes pain. 
Autre maniere d’emplaustre qui restoure les os brisiez: pren foilles de figuier seches, et foilles de pavol terrestre, et broie ensamble, et fai emplaustre.	Another manner of plaster that restores broken bones: take dried fig leaves and ground ivy leaves, and grind them together, and make a plaster.
Autre maniere d’emplaustre a la restoration de la brisure de l’os et por amenusier lou remenant de l’aposteme.	Another manner of treatment for the setting of a broken bone and to lessen the remainder of an abscess. 
Pren racine de vismalve, camomille, flors de violettes, farine de veces, de chescune une partie; et broie, et confis avec vin, et se li membres est eschaufez, se le confis avec aigue de coriandre moiste, ou d’aigue, et en use.	Take root of wormwood, chamomile, flowers of violets, powder of quince, of each one part; and grind, and boil with wine, and if the limb is cold, boil it with moist coriander water, or water, and use it. 
Autre maniere d’emplaustre plus fors que li premier, quant aposteme dur vient a la restauration dou membre.	Another manner of plastering more forceful than the first, when an abscess hardened comes to the restoration of the member.  
Pren racines de vismalve, et semence de lin, balauste, mellilote, et sansuce, et flors de violetes et de camomille, de toutes ana une partie; et broie, et confit avec aigue de salz, ou aigue douce, ou avec vin, selonc la chalor du membre, et son repos.	Take roots of wormwood, and seed of flax, balm, melilot, and marshmallow, and flowers of violets and chamomile, of all one part; and grind, and confit with water of salt, or sweet water, or with wine, according to the heat of the limb, and its rest. 
Ypocras dist en son livre c’on ne mette sus lou membre brisiet por restorer fuer que cerote fait de cire et d’oile, et nulle autre chose plus, et dist qu’il soit entre dous ne trop molz ne trop durs.	Hippocrates says in his book that one should put on the broken limb for restoration only cerate made of wax and oil, and nothing else more, and he says it should be neither too soft nor too hard.
Et il semble galien c’on doit mettre sus lou membre brisiet, por restoreir, choses seches un poc chaudes, si com mirre et aloes et encens et semblans choses, si com nos avons dit.	And it seems Galen one must put on the broken limb, to restore, things dry a little warm, such as myrrh and aloes and incense and similar things, as we have said.
Autre maniere d’emplaustre que vaut a lesion et a dolor: pren mugath et chevolz d’omes decopez, ou pennes d’oiseil, et vismalve, et seil igalment, et les broie, et en fai emplaustre et lou lie.	Another way of making a plaster that is good for the injury and pain: take mugwort and horsetail of sheep cut up, or feathers of eye, and marsh mallow, and silk equally, and grind them together, and make it into a plaster and apply it.
Et puis lou deslie un poc aprés, et pren garde se li malaides ne sent nule dolor, ne ne s’est meus li os de son leu; si ne lou deslie par mains jors Et se dolor vient au leu, ou prurite, sens repos ou enfleure, si te haste, et le deslie, et oste l’emplaustre maintenant; et pren drapeil legier, et esponge de mer moiste, et plonge en aigue, et en leve lou leu, tant que li prurite cesse, et soit apaisie li dolor; puis laisse reposer lou membre une houre, puis met sus lainne seche, moillie en aigue ou en oile roset, et lie sus une nut, tant que tu soies seur d’aposteme chaut; et repose li enflammation dou membre, et soit ostee li dolor, puis rencomence la lieure subtil, et un poc d’emplaustre, et ne l’estraindre mie si fort com devant; et subtilie tant qu’il soit sanez.	And then loosen it a little after, and take care if the patient does not feel any pain, nor have the bones moved from their place; if not, do not loosen it for many days And if pain comes to the place, or itching, no rest, or swelling, so hasten, and loosen it, and remove the plaster now; and take a light cloth, and sponge of sea moist, and dip in water, and raise the place, until the itching ceases, and the pain is calmed; then let the member rest an hour, then put on dry linen, moistened with water or rose oil, and tie up with a nut, as long as you are sure of the hot suppuration; and rest the inflammation of the member, and be the pain removed, then begin again the subtle bandage, and a little plaster, and do not constrain it as hard as before; and continue thus until it is healed. 
Et se tu vois que aposteme chaus et roujor et dolor et enfleure sont jai osteit dou tout, et tu as mestier d’emplaustre et de liien, si le rencomence, si com tu a fait premier igalment.	And if you see that the abscess is hot and red, and pain and swelling have been removed entirely, and you need plaster and bandage, then recommence it, as you did at first equally.
Et s’il ne vient au membre nient de ceu que nos avons dit, si ne le desliier fuer que aprés III jors ou IIII ou V ou VII; et aucune fois le laisse XX jors, selonc ce c’on voit, tant que li briseure s’aert, et aproche li ajostement de la char sus; adonques lou restraint plus que premier, et li engroisse sa viande, si com j’ai dit devant; et se tu vois lou leu sechier plus que mestier n’est, saiches que norrissemens n’i puet venir; arose lou donques d’aigue chaude tede, toutes les fois que tu lou deslies, et ce soit chescun tier jor, et eslaiche un poc la lieure; par cest ovraige donques cort li norrissemens au membre, et esmende hastanment ceu que font li fol mire, que restourent le membre tortement: c’est malvais, et si est ovraiges paourous, et je n’i voi autre chose que dou lassier et de l’ovrer droitement.	And if it does not come to the limb anything of what we have said, then do not unbind it before III days or IV or V or VII; and no time let it XX days, according to what one sees, until the fracture heals, and approaches the adjustment of the bone above; then you restrain it more than first, and make its flesh fat, as I said before; and if you see it drying more than is necessary, know that nourishment cannot come there; rose therefore hot water bath, every time you unbind it, and let it be each third day, and soften a little the flesh; by this work then shortens the nourishment to the limb, and amends quickly what fools do, who leave the limb twisted: it is evil, and so are these works perilous, and I see no other thing than to leave it and to work it straightly. 
des diverses manieres de brisure	of various ways of fracture
Maintes manieres de brisures sont, et diverses figures, et les causes sont destintees; car il a brisures que sont faites de percussion d’espee, et li colz oste tout l’os jusc’a syfac qui est desoz l’os, si com fait haiche ou fust; et por ceu est apelee ceste maniere de briseure «haichure».	Many kinds of fractures there are, and various figures, and the causes are destined; for there are fractures that are made by the percussion of a sword, and the collarbone breaks the entire bone to the point which is below the bone, as it is made with a hatchet or staff; and for this reason this kind of fracture is called «haichure». 
Ou li espee cope l’os par desus sens percier, et est apelee ceste maniere de brisure «absolue»; et est li plaie de ces .ij. brisures grans ou petite.	Where the sword cuts the bone from above without piercing, and this manner of fracture is called "absolute"; and the wound of these two fractures great or small.
Et s’il i a percussion emboee, ou fendure ou de colz de piere, ou de cheoir sus piere, ou d’autres semblans.	And if there is an enclosed percussion, or fissure or of the neck of the bone, or of the cheek on the bone, or similar things.
Et s’il a brisure persant, qui aproiche jusc’a la toile qui est desoz l’os, ou elle est sus l’os, et est ceste plaie ou grans ou petite.	And if there is a fracture passing through, which approaches near the membrane that is beneath the bone, or it is above the bone, and this wound is large or small.
Et si a plaie qui est reponue en l’os ausi subtil com uns chevous, et est li fendure petite, et por ceu est apelee ceste brisure «capillaire»; et si la plaie qui est faite ou par ferir de piere, ou par cheoir, ou par semblant chose, et fait entrer la piece de l’os aval, et est li leus chevez si com font li chaudelier quant il fierent sus les chaudieres; si entre li piere dedens.	And if the wound is laid back in the bone as subtle as a horsehair, and is the fissure small, and for this reason is called this fracture "capillary"; and if the wound which is made either by striking with a stone, or by falling, or by similar thing, and makes enter the piece of bone down, and is the piece like how do the blacksmith when they forge on the cauldrons; so enters the piece into. 
Et ceu avient plus a sous qui ont ou chief moistes os, si com chief d’enfant; et avient a toutes ces manieres de brisure que pieces menues en sont desevrees si com esteles; et je em parlerai en son leu.	And this happens more to those who have or whose chief is moist bone, like the head of a child; and it happens in all these manners of fracture that small pieces are separated from them like stars; and I will speak about this in its place.  
Et conoissons toutes manieres de briseures par la detraction sus elles, et l’inquisition a tentes, et par la char corrumpue qui est sus.	And let us know all the ways of fractures by the traction on them, and the examination of their ends, and by the rotten flesh which is on them.
Li semblance capillaire est conue par ceu que quant on a descovert l’os, et on frote enche sus, si apert li brisure noire.	The appearance of capillary [like] is known by this: when one has uncovered the bone and rubs it above, black fracture appears.
En la cure de la brisure, pren garde as accidens dou malaide, et se tu vois nul malvais accident, si com vomissement de cole, et amission d’entendement, et estension, et paumeson, et fievre ague, et les eulz issir fuer et rougir, et autres semblans em plusors dispositions, si ne puet saner sens doute.	In the cure of the fracture, take heed to the accidents of the patient, and if you see no bad accident, such as vomiting of blood, and loss of understanding, and swelling, and paleness, and fever ague, and eyes red and inflamed, and other similar things in more dispositions, then it cannot heal without doubt.
Et se tu ne vois accident espoantauble, et tu esperes salut, si comence la cure.	And if you do not see an inescapable accident, and you hope for salvation, then begin the treatment.
Et quant li navrez vient premier a toi, et c’est en yver, si covient oster l’os devant lou quatorzime jor au mues que tu pues.	And when the broken bone comes to you first and it is in winter, then it is necessary to remove the bone before the fourteenth day at the time that you can.
Et se c’est en esteit, si te haste d’oster l’os devant lou septime jor, ainsois que li toile desoz l’os soit corrumpue, et vienent li accident que nos avons dit.	And if it is in summer, then hurry to remove the bone before the seventh day, so that the cloth beneath the bone does not rot, and comes the accident that we have said.
Et se li brisure de l’os vient jusc’a la toile que cuevre lou cerveil, si covient que tu fendes la partie fendue ensi: et est que tu preignes lou chief dou malaide navreit, et descuevre sus l’os en toutes les manieres que tu pues, et sus la figure de la plaie plus souef que tu pues, et se decorrement de sanc vient au descovrir l’os, ou aposteme chaus, se li aide de ceu qu’il covient, et est que tu emplisses lou leu de drapelz moilliez en oile roset; et fai reposer la plaie tant que li apostemes chaus soit apaisiez, et soies asseurez de decorrement de sanc; puis comence entor l’os a oster, et c’est fait selonc II manieres d’ovraige: l’une manieres si est que tu copes au taillement estroit devant, et c’est li forme:	And if the bone fracture comes to the membrane that covers the brain, it is necessary that you split the fractured part like this: and it is that you prick the head of the sick person carefully, and uncover on the bone in all ways that you can, and on the figure of the wound more gently than you can, and if blood comes when uncovering the bone, or abscess put, if he helps with what is necessary, and it is that you fill the place with cloths moistened in rose oil; and make the wound rest until the abscess put is appeased, and be sure of blood flow; then begin to remove around the bone, and it is done according to II ways of work: one way is that you cut at a narrow point in front, and this is the form:
Et puis i boute .i. autre taillement plus grant que lou premier.	And then put .i. other incision larger than the first.
Et li seconde maniere si est que tu aies nombre de taillemens divers, les uns estrois et les autres larges, et soient a pointe, et soient de fer inde; et en oevre legierement, que tu ne bleces le chief.	And the second way is that you have a number of incisions of various kinds, some narrow and others wide, and they are pointed, and made of iron; and in working lightly so that you do not break the head.
Et se li os est fors et durs, si covient que tu lou perces entor, ainsois que tu i aministres taillemens avec tareres, ne mie parfont qu’il ne percent outre l’os dou test, et doit avoir desus la pointe une marge ou .i. cercle, que ne laisse passer la pointe outre l’os; et t’en covient avoir plusors, si que chescuns viegne en son leu de l’espés de l’os, si que tu aies tareres por toz test; et c’est li forme des .iij. manieres de tareres:	And if the bone is hard and stiff, it is necessary that you pierce it inward, so that you administer splints with drills, lest they go beyond the bone of the test, and it must have above the tip a margin or circle, that does not allow the tip to pass beyond the bone; and you should have several, so that each one comes in its place from the thickness of the bone, so that you have drills for all tests; and this is the form of the three kinds of drills:
Li qualitez por percier entor l’os brisiet est que tu mettes .i. tarere sus l’os, et torne a tes dois tant que tu saiches que li os est perciez; puis remet lou tarere autre part un poc aprés, et ensi fai tout entor, puis cope as incisoires l’espaice de l’os qui est entre les pertu, et fai ceu plus subtilment que tu pues, et legierement, si com nos avons dit, tant que tu en traies l’os a ta main, ou a aucun des estrumens que tu as si com tenailles, pinceures; et garde que tu ne touchier au cerveil ne au syfac.	The qualities for piercing inward the broken bone is that you put one drill on the bone, and turn to your two hands until you know that the bone is pierced; then put the drill elsewhere a bit afterwards, and so do all around, then cut with the incisors the space of the bone that is between the perforations, and do this more subtly than you can, and lightly, as we have said, until you draw the bone to your hand, or to any of the instruments that you have like pliers; and keep that you do not touch the marrow nor the surface.
Et quant tu avras l’os perciet, et trait, si couvient que tu reses l’apreteit qui est demoree de l’os a tout I autre estrument semblant a un incisoire, mais il covient qu’il soit plus tenves et plus subtilz que li autre incisoire; et s’il demore rien des petis os, ne nulles escailles, si les oste legierement a I des estrumens; puis cure la plaie de tentes et d’oignemens que nos avons dit.	And when you will have pierced the bone, and treated it, it is necessary that you reset the apparatus which is stayed away from the bone to all the other instruments seeming like an incisor, but it is necessary that it be more firm and more subtle than the other incisor; and if there remains nothing of the small bones, nor any scales, then cure the wound lightly with the instruments; then cure the wound with tents and ointments as we have said. 
Et li autre maniere d’ovraige si est cil que Galiiens dist, qui est plus saus; et le loa molt fort, et c’est sa parole.	And the other manner of work is that which Galen says, which is drier; and it is very strong, and this is his word.
Il covient premier que tu descuevres une partie de l’os, lai ou li brisure apert mues, si que quant tu avras descovert celle partie, si metras desoz lou chief de cest incisoire lenticulaire, de coi ci est li forme:	It is necessary first that you uncover a part of the bone, where the fracture appears open, so that when you have uncovered that part, you will place beneath the head of this lentil-shaped incision, which is the shape:
Et ne trenche mie d’une part, et est li partie ague en II costez, si que li trenchans est devers l’os; puis fier d’un petit maillet sus, si que tu copes legierement l’os tout entor, et te done bien en garde de la toile que est desoz; s’elle est aerse a l’os, si l’oste legierement, car on l’oste bien sens nuisement, et sens paor.	And do not cut from one part, and is the piece acute in II costs, so that the trenchans is towards the bone; then strike of a small hammer up, so that you lightly chop the bone all around, and give yourself well guard of the cloth that is below; if it is hard to the bone, so lightly the bone, because one lightly bones without nuisance, and without fear.   
Et se li brisure de l’os ne perce jusc’a la toile, et li os est copez tant soulement par desus, et il i a petites pieces, si couvient rere celle asperiteit, et oster les escailles au raseur subtil dont tu dois avoir diverses quantitez, por amenistrer en chescun leu le plus covenauble, selonc l’ovraige et la figure de la brisure.	And if the broken bone does not pierce to the skin, and the bone is only cut from above, and there are small pieces, then it is necessary to put this asperity back together, and remove the scales with a fine razor which you must have various quantities of, in order to apply in each place the most suitable one, according to the work and the figure of the fracture.
Si reis devant de grant raseurs, et aprés de plus subtilz, et ne cesser de ceu faire tant k’il soit bien.	If you begin with large razors, and afterwards with finer ones, and do not cease to do this until it is well.
En chescune des autres tailleures capillaires et brisure subtil, covient que tu amenistres cause selonc son adrecement; et c’est chose que n’est mie reponue a celui qui a mis son estude en cest art.	In each of the other capillary cuts and subtle fractures, it is necessary that you apply the cause according to its appropriateness; and this is something that is not well-repaired to him who has put his study into this art.
Et se li syfac est descovers, si covient que tu preignes I drapeil de lin, selonc la quantiteit de la plaie, et le moille en oile roset, et le mete sus la plaie; puis pren I autre drapeil doublé en II ou en III, et le moille en vin et en oile roset, et le met sus le premier drapeil; et fai ceu plus legierement que tu pues, que tu ne grever lou syfac, puis aministre par desus I autre liien, et ne l’estraindre mie fuer que tant k’il teigne la plaie soulement; et le laisse ensi I jor ou II, tant que li apostemes chaus soit apaisiez, puis le deslie, et i aministre medicines si com racines de lis, et farine de veces, et poudre d’encens, et aristologe, et semblans choses; et de ces choses fai poudre, et met sus la plaie, et i aministre adés medicines mondefians sens mordication; et warde que li plaie soit adés nette, que tu n’i laissier mie l’oignement, ne n’i laissier mie assembler bowe, car quant bowe s’asemble sus la toile dou cerveil, si la corront et porrist, et avient au malaide grans malice; car quant on met sus lou syfac chose mordant, si nercist par desus.	And if the wound is open, you must take a linen cloth, according to the quantity of the wound, and soak it in rose oil, and place it on the wound; then take another cloth doubled in two or three, and soak it in wine and rose oil, and place it on top of the first cloth; and do this more lightly than you can, that you do not damage the skin; then administer a bandage from above, and do not bind it too tightly except as long as it holds the wound only; and leave it so for one or two days, until the abscess has subsided, then untie it, and administer medicines such as roots of lilies, and flour of violets, and incense powder, and ariadne, and similar things; and make powder from these things, and place it on the wound, and administer additional medications without biting; and guard that the wound is always clean, that you do not leave any oil, nor allow it to gather pus, for when pus gathers on the cloth of the brain, it rots and irritates, and brings great harm to the patient; for when one puts a stinging thing on the skin, it stings from above. 
Pren warde donques, se nulz mavais accidens vient au malaide, si com nos avons dit, si est doutable sens doute.	Take guard therefore, if no bad accidents come to the patient, as we have said, then it is doubtful without doubt.
Et se noiror n’i vient fuers que por la medicine c’on a mise sus, que teil vertu ait, si covient que tu preignes une partie de miel, et III parties d’oile roset et en glue I drapel, et lou met sus lou syfac; puis lou cure de manieres de cures qu’il couvient, tant qu’il soit sanez.	And if the pus does not come out except for the medicine that has been put on it, that it has virtue, then it is necessary that you take a part of honey and III parts of rose oil and glue one cloth, and place it on the wound; then cure it in ways of cures as it is necessary, until it is healed. 
Saiches que aucune fois sont brisies les .ij. parties dou neis desus, ou li une par li soulement, car ce sont .ij. os; et cil desoz est cartillaginous, et ne brise mie, si ne fait fuer k’enbower ou tordre, et puis saine.	Know that sometimes the two parts of the bone above are broken, or one by itself, because they are two bones; and the lower one is cartilaginous, and does not break, unless it is forced to bend or twist, and then heals.
Et se l’une des II parties est brisie, si covient que tu boutes ton petit doi ou pertus dou neis, et met igal cele brisure dedens a ton doi indice, et au pouchet par defuer, tant que li neis reveigne selonc sa figure natureil; et fai ce legierement, et warde que li malaides ne sente dolor par ton ovraige.	And if one of the two parts is broken, it behooves you to put your small finger or through the two, and place each fracture within your index finger, and in the palm outwards, until the two returns according to its natural figure; and do this gently, and watch that the patient does not feel pain from your work. 
Et se li brisure est en la partie desus lou neis, et ne puet venir li dois au leu, si couvient que tu lou mettes igal au chief d’un rait un poc gros.	And if the fracture is in the part above the knees, and it cannot come to its place, then you must put it equal to the head of a rail, a piece thick.
Et se li brisure est de II pars, si refai autretel, et te haste de restorer lou premier jor, se tu pues; se ce non, aprés lou septime ou le disime, quant li apostemes chaus est apaisiez, puis bouthe au chief dou neis tentes de drapelz de lin, se li brisure est en une partie.	And if the fracture is of two parts, make another splint, and hasten to restore it on the first day, if you can; if not, after the seventh or tenth, when the abscess has calmed, then band it at the head with two layers of linen cloth, if the fracture is in one part. 
Ou tu boutes .i. autre d’autre part, se li brisure i est; et soient les tentes si grosses qu’eles emplissent les pertus dou neis.	Or you put one on the other side, if the fracture is there; and let the bandages be so thick that they fill the hollows of the knee.
Et dirent aucun restorour c’on doit moillier les tentes em burre, et muer chescun jor.	And said any restorer one must moisten the tents with butter, and change each day.  
Il ne me semble mie que ce soit bien, mais covient qu’elles soient moillies en confection d’aubun d’uef, et de poudre de molin; puis laisse la tente, tant que li os soit fers, et li cartilaiges endurcis.	It does not seem to me that this is good, but it should be moistened in the making of auburn from an egg, and powdered millet; then let the tent remain until the bone is hard, and the cartilage hardened.  
Et aucune fois boute on ou neis pennes d’oie en leu de tentes, et si a .i. souef drapeil entor, et se tient la briseure, et soffle li malaides parmi; or fai lou kel ke tu vues.	And sometimes one puts goose feathers in their place, and if it is i.e., a single sheet of cloth, wrapped around, and the breeze holds it, and blows on the patient; or do as you please.
Et se apostemes chaus vient au neis tandis com tu i oevres, si i met cerote ou coton moilliet en oile roset, ou en .i. poc de dyaquilon.	And if an abscess comes to the boil while you are working on it, put a certain kind of cloth or cotton moistened in rose oil, or in i. p. (one part) of dyacylon.
Et se apostemes chaus n’i vient, si met emplastre par defuer, de farine de sisament, et de poudre d’encens, confites avec aubun d’uef, puis met sus estoupes legieres, et ne liier mie lou neis; et se li brisure dou neis est petite, et il est brisiés em menues pieces, si l’estrai as estrumens covenables a ceu, puis cout la fendure, et la cure de choses enjanrans char, et soudant, si com emplastres et oignemens covenables a ceu; et s’il n’a dedens lou neis nule plaie, si lou cure de tentes, et i met cainnes de plonc, tant qu’il soit sanez.	And if abscesses do not come, then put a plaster from outside, of sesame flour and incense powder, cooked with yolk of egg, then put light compresses on top and bind it not at all nor the sides; and if the fracture is small and broken in small pieces, then extract it with suitable instruments, then sew the opening, and cure it with things like flesh and sutures, as well as plasters and ointments as are suitable for it; and if there is no wound inside, then cure it with tents, and put twigs of plantain in it, until it is healed.  
de la routure de la mandibule	Of the fracture of the jawbone.
Quant li mandibule est route desoz, et il n’i a point de plaie defuer, et li brisure est defuer tant soulement, et n’est mie brisiez en dous, fuer que corbelz dedens, si le conoist on legierement.	When the jaw is broken from below and there is no wound outside and the fracture is outside only, and is not broken in two, except for the corner inside, if one knows it lightly.
Il covient, se li brisure est en la destre partie, que tu boutes ton doi indice en la bouche dou malaide; et se li brisure est en la mandibule senestre, si boute ton doi indice de la main destre, et boute la brisure dedens legierement, et l’afaite a l’autre main par defuer, et met igal.	It is necessary, if the fracture is in the right side, that you put your right index finger into the mouth of the patient; and if the fracture is in the left mandible, then put your right index finger, and push the fracture inward lightly, and hold it with the other hand outside, and make it even.
Et se li brisure de la mandibule est desevree en .ij., si covient que tu aministres extension de .ij. pars, tant qu’il soit igalz.	And if the jaw fracture is separated into two, it is necessary that you administer extension to two parts, until it is even.
Et se commovemens des dens avient, ou routure, si les estrain d’un fil d’or ou d’argent ou de soie, puis met sus la mandibule brisie cerote, et met sus .i. drapel double; et met sus lou drapel une grant estele bien faite, ou une piece de cuir igal, longe et large selonc la mandibule, et la lie sus au mues que tu pues, et comande a malaide qu’il se repose, et menjuce viandes legieres.	And if movement of the teeth occurs, or rupture, then strain with a thread of gold or silver or silk, then put on the broken jaw cerote, and put on i. drapel double; and put on lou drapel a great estele well made, or a piece of leather equal, long and wide according to the mandible, and tie it up as much as you can, and command the patient to rest, and eat light meats.
Et se tu vois que li figure se chainge, si le deslie au tier jor, et puis remet a point ceu que c’est chaingiet; et met emplaustre sus, de la poudre dou moulin avec abun d’uef ou farine de sisament, aprés ceu que tu as osteit lou cerote; et met sus l’emplaustre lainne legiere, et se li emplaustres s’aert, et li disposition dou malaide ne se chainge, laisse le tant k’il soit sanez; maintes fois est sanez en .iij. semainnes.	And if you see that the figure changes, then untie it on the third day, and then put back what is changed; and put a plaster above, of powder from the mill with an abundance of egg or sesame flour, after what you have removed the cerote; and put on the plaster light linen, and if the plasters adhere, and the disposition of the patient does not change, leave it as long as it is healthy; many times is healed in iii. weeks.
Et se apostemes chaus vient, si amenistre ceu que nos avons dit maintes fois.	And if an abscess comes, then administer what we have said many times.
Et s’il i a brisure et plaie ensamble, rewarde se nule piece de l’os en est ostee; si subtilie de l’oster as estrumens covenaubles a ceu.	And if there is a fracture and wound together, reward no piece of the bone in it is removed; so subtle of the remove with instruments suitable to this.
Et se li plaie est estroite, si l’eslarge a flaime selonc ta necessiteit.	And if the wound is narrow, so enlarge it with flame according to your need.
Et quant tu avras ostees toutes ces pieces, et il n’i demore nule, si cout la plaie, s’ele est large, et met sus .i. des emplaustres covenaubles a ceu, por enjanrer char, tant qu’il soit sanez.	And when you will have removed all these pieces, and there remains none, then cover the wound, if it is wide, and put upon it one plaster suitable to this, to prevent flesh, until it is healed.
de la forcele	of the forceps
Li forcele brise plusors fois devant, devers l’espaule, et li brisure si est selonc .iij. manieres: ou elle est brisie et desevree en .ij., et i a pieces, et est li plus legiere a restorer; ou il i a brisure avec plaie.	The fracture breaks many times before, towards the shoulder, and the fracture is according to .iij. manners: or it is broken and severed in .ij., and there are pieces, and it is the lightest to restore; or there is a fracture with a wound.
Et li ovraiges si est quant li brisure est sens plaie, que tu aies .ij. ministres, si que li uns teigne l’ajutoire que sent la forcele brisie; et li autres estende lou col vers la partie; puis met igal la brisure a tes dois, si que li forme soit si com il covient, et n’i ait ne apparance ne chevement.	And the work is when the fracture is without a wound, that you have .ij. ministers, so that one holds the shoulder which is the fractured bone; and the other extends the neck towards the part; then put equal the fracture to your hands, so that the form is as it should be, and there is no appearance nor blemish.
Et se tu a mestier de plus estendre, si met desoz l’espaule au malaide une spere reonde de drap, ou de lainne, et soit grosse selonc ta necessiteit; et estent et lieve la forcele, et estraint le spere a ta main, si que tu mettes igal la brisure, si com il covient.	And if you need to extend it more, put under the shoulder of the patient a round sphere of cloth, or wool, and be thick according to your necessity; and being and lift the fracture, and restrain the sphere in your hand, so that you put equal the fracture, as it should be.
Et se tu ne pues traire le chief de la forcele au defuer, por ceu qu’elle vient em parfont, si covient que li malaides gise sovin sus son hatereil, et met desoz l’espaule .i. oreillier moien, et boute li ministres l’espaule aval, tant que li os de la forcele soit levez.	And if you cannot draw the head of the shoulder with force because it comes in front, then it is necessary that the patient lie often on his back and place under the shoulder one hour glass, and put the ministers the shoulder down, until the bone of the shoulder be lifted.
Et puis adrece la brisure a tes dois, et se tu lou trueves debrisiet, si covient que tu fendes sus; et trai fuer les pieces legierement, et se li os ne puet joindre por aucun sormontement, si le cope a l’un des incisoires que tu as; mais que tu mettes desoz la forcele cest estrument, por warder lou syfac:	And then direct the fracture to your two hands, and if you find it broken, then it is necessary that you split it up; and treat the pieces gently, and if the bones cannot join for any mounting, then cut it at one of the incisors that you have; but put under the shoulder this instrument, in order to ward off the syfac:
et semble une cuiller, et n’est mie chevez, et soit ausi large com il lou covient, selonc la grandor de l’os et la possance de l’uevre; et ait II chiés, si com tu vois, et soit li uns larges, et li autres estrois, si com est li routure de la plaie que tu as fait quant tu as trait les os; et quant tu es asseurez d’aposteme chaut, si cout la plaie, et se li routure est petite, ou tu doutes aposteme chaut, si emple la plaie de drapelz et de tentes, selonc sa quantiteit.	and it appears like a spoon, and is not entirely hollow, and be as wide as it is fitting, according to the size of the bone and the force of the work; and it has two handles, as you see, and one is wide, and the other narrow, as is the rupture of the wound that you made when you treated the bones; and when you are assured of a hot abscess, so stitch up the wound, and if the rupture is small, or you doubt about a hot abscess, then apply the wound with bandages and tents, according to its quantity. 
Et se apostemes chaus i vient, si moille .i. drapel en oile roset et en aisil et en vin, et met sus.	And if the hot abscess comes, then moisten one bandage in rose oil and honey and wine, and put it on top.
Li estrignement de l’os, quant il est sens plaies et sens tailleure, est que tu mettes sus l’os l’enplaustre de la poudre dou molin et d’aubun d’uef, et met estoupes legieres sus, et met une spere desoz l’assele, se tu en as mestier; puis pren une tres grant bende d’une palme de large, puis met I drapeil double sus estoupes, et l’emplaustre; puis pren une estele subtil de III dois de large et de lonc, et l’envoloupe entor celui bendeil, si que li chief dou bendeil soient pendant; et met l’estele sus la briseure, et torne le bendeil sus lou col, et desoz l’asselle sainne, et desoz l’asselle malaide; et la ramoinne de chescune partie, et quant tu vois ke li lieure tient la briseure avenanment, et tu vois clerement que li estele ne se muet desus l’os brisiet, si le rewarde chescun jor, et quant li liiens eslaiche, si lou restraint; et fai gesir lou malaide sus son dos, et li met quant il dort I petit orillier desoz l’assele, por lever l’ajutoire en haut; si aligera li brisure de la forcele par l’aligement de l’espaule, ou tu li lie lou bras au col, et ne lou desliier se malvais accidens n’i vient, jusc’a XX jors; puis rencomence l’emplaustre, se tu vois qu’il en ait mestier, et l’estraint et le laisse ensi, tant qu’il soit restorez; et elle est restoree au plus en XXVIII jors, et en moins a aucuns homes.	The tightening of the bone, when it is without wounds and without cutting, is that you put upon the bone the plaster of the powder of the mill and of albumen of egg, and put light poultices upon, and put a spear under the ass-hole, if you have need; then take a very great bandage of one palm wide, then put the double drape upon the poultices, and the plaster; then take a strip subtle of III fingers wide and long, and envelop it around that bandaged, so that the head of the band be hanging; and put the strip upon the fracture, and turn the bandage upon the neck, and under the healthy ass-hole, and under the broken ass-hole; and tighten each part, and when you see that the ligature holds the fracture well, and you clearly see that the strip does not move above the broken bone, so reward each day, and when the ligatures slacken, so restrain it; and make the patient lie upon his back, and put him when he sleeps the small pillow under the ass-hole, to raise the shoulder upwards; thus alleviate the fracture of the force by the lightening of the shoulder, or you bind the arm to the neck, and do not untie it unless bad accidents come to pass, until XX days; then recommence the plaster, if you see that he has need, and restrain and leave him so, until it be restored; and it is restored at most in XXVIII days, and less for some men. 
de la brisure de l’espaule	of the breaking of the shoulder
Poc avient que li espaule brise ou large leu ne em mileu, et ne lou seit on fuers que par lou touchement; si l’apareille selonc la figure de la brisure, et la ramoinne selonc sa forme natureil, en toutes les manieres que tu pues; puis met sor le leu poudre dou molin avec abun d’uef, et estoupes legieres, et met sus I drapeil double; puis met sus une estele large et subtil, un poc plus large de l’espaule, et se desoz l’estele est chevemens en aucun leu de l’espaule, si emple celle cheveure d’estoupes de lin, si que li estele siece igalment; et puis l’estraint a tout une grant bende fermement, si que li estele ne se mueve de son leu, et regarde le liien chescun jor; et toutes les fois k’il amolist, si l’estraint, et met igal l’estele s’elle est fuer de son leu, et gise li malaides sus son costeit; et li espaule est restouree en XX jors ou en xxv, Et quant cist nombres est acomplis, si le deslie, et soies seur, car ce n’est mie os qui se mette fuer legierement.	It happens that the shoulder breaks or its large bone is in the middle, and it is not felt other than by touch; therefore, arrange the apparatus according to the shape of the fracture, and set it according to its natural form, in all the ways you can; then put on the place powder of the mill with abundance of egg, and light bandages, and put a double cloth above; then put on top a wide and fine plate, a little wider than the shoulder, and if the plate is chafed at any place on the shoulder, use that chafing with lin bandages, so that the plate rests equally; and then restrain it with a very strong bandage tightly, so that the plate does not move from its place, and look at it every day; and every time it softens, bind it again, and put the plate back in its place if it is out of place, and lie the patient on his side; and the shoulder will be restored in 20 days or in 25, and when this number is completed, then untie it, and be sure, because it is not a bone that puts itself out easily.
Et se une piece de l’os tent avant, et point desoz lou keur, si fent sus, et l’oste, et fai ceu que nos avons dit en la brisure de la forcele d’apaisier aposteme chaut.	And if a piece of the bone comes before, and not under the heart, so make it up, and the bone, and do what we have said in the breaking of the collarbone to soothe an abscess hot.
de la brisure dou pis	of the fracture of the leg
Li peis est aucune fois brisiez em mileu, mais ce avient poc; mais il brise sovent au defuer.	The bone is sometimes broken in the middle, but this happens little; but it often breaks at the end. 
Et quant il est brisiez emmi, si decline au desoz, et sent li malaides grant dolor, et faute d’alainne, et toust, et par aventure vomist sanc, et apert li chevemens en l’os brisiés, et lou sent om bien.	And when it is broken in the middle, it sinks to the groin, and the patient feels great pain, and lack of strength, and all, and perhaps vomits blood, and opens the joints in the broken bone, and he feels it well.  
Et li restoracion si est que li malaides gise sovin sus son dos, et li met .i. cussin entre les espaules, puis presse les espaules, et li assemble les costes des .ij. costelz a tes mains, et subtilie dou mettre igal a toutes les manieres que tu pues, tant que li figure de l’os soit si com il covient; puis met sus emplaustres et estoupes desus l’estele subtil de salz, ou d’autre fust legier, et l’envoloupe en .i. drapeil, et la lie sus l’os brisiet, si qu’elle ne se mueve; et torne le liien reondement au dos, et l’estraint avenanment, et rewarde chescune houre la liure, et s’elle ramolist, si l’estraint; et s’il lou covient desliier, por corrosion ou por prurite que vient au leu, ou dolor ou aposteme, si te haste, et lou deslie, et raie l’emplaustre, et adresce ceu qui i est venus de manieres de cures; puis ramoinne l’emplaustre, et l’estraint tant qu’il soit sanez.	And the restoration is that the patient lies often on his back, and one cushion is put between the shoulders, then the shoulders are pressed, and the sides of the two bones are brought together with your hands, and subtly in all ways as you can, until the shape of the bone is as it should be; then bandages and poultices are put on top of a thin layer of salt, or another light plaster, and it is wrapped in one cloth, and laid upon the broken bone, so that it does not move; and the bandage is turned around back and forth, and kept tight, and each hour the bandage is checked, and if it loosens, it is tightened; and if it needs to be loosened for corrosion or itching that comes to the place, or pain or abscess, then hurry, and loosen it, and wipe off what has come out in ways of cures; then reapply the bandage, and keep it tight until it is healed.
de la brisure des costes	of the breaking of the ribs
Saiches que li briseure des costes ne vient fuer que en leus gros aprés le dos, et au chief dedens, et ne ploient fuer que por ceu qu’eles sont glairouses; et le sent om bien a l’inquisition as dois.	Know that the breaking of the ribs comes only in their thick parts after the back, and at the head within, and they bend only for this reason because they are glassy; and one feels it well upon inquiry on the sides.
Et li restoremens si est que tu met igal la brisure, si com il covient.	And the restoration is that you put each fracture equal as it befits. 
Et puis met l’emplaustre, et l’estraint d’une estele, se tu en as mestier.	And then place the splint, and restrain it with a strap, if you need it. 
Et se li brisure des costes est declinans au dedens, si sent li malaides tres grant dolor, et pointure, ausi com de pleuresin, car li os point la toile; et alaine a poinnes, et toust et vomist grant planteit de sanc, et c’est de grief santeit, et li ancien i ont mis grant engien.	And if the breaking of the ribs is sloping inwards, the patient feels very great pain and stabbing, as well as shortness of breath, because the bone pricks the lung; and also, they have pains, and cough and vomit blood greatly, and this is a serious illness, and the ancients put much importance on it.
Et si a de ces qui dient qu’il le covient aministrer viandes qui enflent le ventre, por bouter la briseure au defuer.	And if there are some who say that it is necessary to administer meats which swell the belly, in order to expel the wind outward.
Nos ne faisons mie ceu, que apostemes chaulz n’i viegne, s’il n’i est venus.	We do not do this, except for abscesses that have come warm.
Et s’il i est venus, si le saine.	And if it has come, treat it.
Et dirent aucun d’ous c’on met ventouses sor le leu, por sucier forment, et c’est semblant a raison; toutevoie, doutance i a que li ventouse n’atraie les superfluitez au leu por la disposition de foiblece.	And some of you say that one should put suction cups on the spot, to suck out forcefully, and this seems reasonable; however, there is doubt that the suction cup attracts superfluities at the site for the sake of weakness.
Et dirent aucun d’ous c’on doit covrir le leu de lainne moillie en oile chaut, et met on tentes entre les costes por metre igal la lieure entor, puis soit curez de la cure de pleurisin, de viande et de medicine.	And they say none of you should cover the place with wool soaked in hot oil, and put a tent between the ribs to make the area even, then be cured from the treatment of pleurisy, meat, and medicine.
Et se li malaides sent grant dolor qu’il ne puet soffrir par l’os qui point la toile, et doutance est au malaide, si covient fendre sus lou leu de la coste brisie, et covient mettre desoz I estrument por warder lou syfac; et adonques doit on trenchier l’os, et traire legierement, et puis coudre les II levres de la plaie, s’elle est grans, et le curez d’emplaustres, tant qu’il soit sanez.	And if the patient feels great pain that he cannot bear from the bone which points to the cloth, and there is doubt in the patient, then it is necessary to split above the place of the broken rib, and it is necessary to put underneath an instrument for guarding the syfac; and afterwards one must cut into the bone, and gently draw (it), and then sew the two lips of the wound, if it is great, and cure it with ointments, until it is healed.  
Et se apostemes chaus vient, si met sus drapelz moilliez en oile roset, et met sus lou leu; et cure tandis le leu de ceu qu’il covient a aposteme, et soit gisans sovins sus lou costeit ou il doit, tant qu’il soit sanez.	And if an abscess comes, put on it soft cloths soaked in rose oil, and put on top of it; and cure as long as the place of the abscess is needed, and lying flat on his side or where it must be, until it is healed.  
de la brisure dou col	of the fracture of the neck
Aucune fois vient briseure as os dou col, et poc avient k’il ploie; et ausi a l’espinee dou dos.	Sometimes a fracture comes to the bone of the neck, and sometimes it happens that it bends; and also in the spine of the back.
Et quant ce avient a aucun, et tu vues savoir s’il sera sanez ou non, si pren warde se tu vois ses mains mollifiies et enflees et mortes, et ne les puet movoir ne estendre, et quant tu les poins d’une aguille, si ne lou sent.	And when this happens to anyone, and you want to know if he will be healed or not, then take ward if you see his hands softened and swollen and dead, and he cannot move them or stretch them, and when you prick them with a needle, he does not feel it.  
Saiches qu’il est mortelz em plusors dispositions, et s’il les muet et sent bien quant on les estraint et point, saiches que li jointure de l’os est sauve, et que li malaides iert curez, s’on i met cure.	Know that it is mortal in many positions, and if they move and feel well when they are pulled and pointed, know that the joint of the bone is saved, and that the patient will be cured, if one puts care to it. 
Et se semblans chose vient a l’espinee dou dos, et tu vues savoir s’il iert sanez ou non, si pren warde se li piez sont amollis, et se li accident i sont qui sont em mains, et sus lou dos saut ventositeit et egestion sens volonteit; et quant on l’a sovineit sus son ventre, si saut egestion sens volonteit.	And if such things happen to the spine of the back, and you want to know if it is healthy or not, if you see that the feet are soft, and if the accident there are in hands, and on the back there is windiness and ejection without will; and when one supports it on its belly, it ejects without will. 
Et quant il est sovinez sus son dos et il vuet asseller, si ne puet.	And when he is healed on his back and wants to sit up, he cannot.  
Saiches qu’il est mortelz: si ne laboreir mie en sa cure.	Know that it is deadly if you do not labor in its care. 
Et se cist accident ne li vienent, si est li chose plus legiere.	And if this accident does not come to him, it is the lighter thing.
Et li cure si est que tu rewardes s’il i a aposteme chaut, si met sus l’espinee oile roset ou oile de moieuf d’uef, tant qu’il soit apaisiez; puis met sus un des emplaustres confortans et sechans, et le lie d’un liien, et comande au malaide a reposer; et ne dorme mie sus celle partie ou il sent la dolor, tant k’il soit sanez.	And the cure is that you reward if there is an abscess hot, then put on the spine rose oil or the oil of egg yolk, until it be appeased; then put on a comforting and drying plaster, and bind it with a bandage, and command the patient to rest; and let him not sleep on that part where he feels pain, as long as he is sound.
Et se aucuns os se dessevre, si covient coper sus lou keur; et oste l’os, puis assemble les .ij. levres de la plaie, s’elle est grans, a une costure, puis le cure d’oignement enjanrant char, tant qu’il soit sanez; et se li chiés de l’os est brisiez, ki est apelez li «chiés de la kowe», se i boute ton pouchet de ta main senestre, et le met igal a l’autre main a ton pooir, puis met l’emplaustre et l’estele sus, se tu en a mestier; puis l’estraint, et s’il i a os desevreit, si fent sus, et l’oste, et cure la plaie tant qu’elle soit sanee.	And if any bone is broken, it behoves to cover on the heart; and take out the bone, then assemble the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with one suture, then cure it with ointment that promotes flesh, until it is sound; and if the head of the bone is broken, which is called the “head of the cow,” put your left hand pouch into it, and set it equal to the other hand as best you can, then put the plaster and splint on top, if you need it; then restrain it, and if there are bones dislocated, do so again, take them out, and cure the wound until it is sound.
de la brisure de l’os de la hainche	of the fracture of the hip bone
Poc avient que li os de la hainche soit brisiez, et s’il avient, si n’avient fuer c’un poc d’amenuisement au chief; et fent dou lonc, et decline au pardedens, et vient au malaide dolor au leu, et pointure, et enfle ceu que brise.	It happens that the bone of the hip is broken, and if it happens, there should not have been much decrease at the top; and making it long, and declining to the side, and comes to the patient pain at the place, and prickling, and swells what breaks.
